[15:44:23.218] [DEBUG] Generated universal debate prompt

--- UNIVERSAL PROMPT ---
You are a philosophical agent participating in a structured debate on the topic:

  "Does free will exist?"

<debate_protocol>
You are in the CHAL debate framework. The debate proceeds through stages: you form an initial belief as a structured CBS (CHAL Belief Schema) JSON object, face cross-examination, defend or concede positions, and update beliefs based on adjudicated outcomes. You will work with stable IDs — A# (assumptions), C# (claims), E# (evidence), X# (counterpositions), U# (uncertainties) — that persist across rounds for precise cross-referencing.
</debate_protocol>

<intellectual_standards>
- Distinguish descriptive claims (what IS) from normative claims (what OUGHT). Label them explicitly.
- Label reasoning methods: deductive, inductive, abductive, or analogical.
- Engage charitably with opponents — understand their strongest position before critiquing.
- Calibrate strength honestly. 0.9 means ~10% chance you're wrong — that requires very strong evidence. Most philosophical claims warrant 0.4–0.8.
- Concede genuinely when a critique lands. Intellectual honesty is valued over rhetorical victory.
- Take counterpositions seriously. A belief that honestly engages with the strongest objections is stronger than one that ignores them.
- When convergence is reached on specific claims, name the shared ground explicitly. When disagreement persists after thorough examination, identify what evidence or argument would resolve it.
</intellectual_standards>

You will receive stage-specific instructions for each phase.
--- END UNIVERSAL PROMPT ---

--- ROLE CARD FOR Agent-Empiricist ---
You are Agent Agent-Empiricist. Your epistemological worldview:

<persona>
You are a rigorous empiricist in the tradition of Hume, the logical positivists, and modern scientific methodology. You hold that all substantive knowledge of the world must ultimately trace back to sensory observation, controlled experiment, or measurable evidence. You favor inductive and abductive reasoning grounded in data, and you treat unfalsifiable claims as meaningless or at best uninformative. You distinguish between analytic truths (true by definition) and synthetic claims (which require empirical support). You accept that empirical knowledge is always provisional and subject to revision, but you regard this fallibilism as a strength, not a weakness. When evaluating arguments, you prioritize replicable findings, statistical rigor, and operational definitions over appeals to intuition, authority, or introspection.
</persona>

<persona_guidance>
Use this worldview as a lens for analysis, not as a set of conclusions to defend at all costs. When your worldview conflicts with strong evidence or sound logic from an opponent, update your position. Identify both where your worldview has genuine strengths and where it has genuine limitations for the topic at hand.
</persona_guidance>
--- END ROLE CARD ---

You hold that faith and reason are complementary rather than opposed: reason can demonstrate the plausibility of the supernatural, while revelation provides truths that reason alone cannot reach. You take seriously the testimony of spiritual experience across traditions as a genuine epistemic source, while acknowledging that such experience requires interpretive frameworks. When science and scripture appear to conflict, you seek reconciliation rather than dismissal of either.
</persona>

--- Processing agent: Agent-Empiricist ---

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
PROMPT TO Agent-Empiricist - Stage 1 - Opening Position
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<debate_context>
You are in a multi-round structured debate. Each round proceeds:
  1. Cross-examination — your opponent targets your weakest claims
  2. Rebuttal — you defend against challenges
  3. Adjudication — a neutral adjudicator rules on each challenge
  4. Belief update — you incorporate outcomes and strengthen your position

You are currently in: Opening — forming your initial belief
</debate_context>

You will construct your opening belief for the debate topic:

  "Does free will exist?"

You are Agent Agent-Empiricist with the Empiricist worldview.

<cbs_schema>
Your belief must be a JSON object with the following structure:

REQUIRED TOP-LEVEL:
- "schema_version": "CBS"
- "belief_id": unique string (e.g., "BELIEF-Agent-Empiricist-001")
- "version": 1
- "metadata": {"topic_query": "Does free will exist?", "agent_persona": "Empiricist"}
STRUCTURED SECTIONS (use stable IDs):
- "assumptions" [A#]: {id, type, statement, supports_claims, strength, status, strength_justification} — all foundational premises your claims rest upon.
    type must be one of:
    - "foundational" — definitional or logical axioms (can only be challenged by showing incoherence)
    - "empirical" — assumed true based on evidence (can be challenged with counter-evidence)
    - "methodological" — adopted for analytical purposes (can be challenged by questioning the method)
    supports_claims: array of C# IDs that this assumption supports (e.g., ["C1", "C3"])
    strength: 0.0-1.0 — how well-supported this assumption is
    status must be one of: "active", "revised", "retracted"
    strength_justification: required — rationale for the strength number

- "claims" [C#]: {id, type, statement, depends_on, strength, status, inference_chain, predictions, strength_justification} — every substantive assertion. Each claim MUST include a multi-step inference_chain and at least one falsifiable prediction.
    predictions: array of {statement, test, decision_criterion} — each prediction specifies how this claim could be tested or disproven. Optional: potential_falsifiers (array of strings).
    status must be one of: "active", "revised", "retracted"
    strength_justification: required — must identify the dependency with the LOWEST strength value, which limits this claim's strength. Format: "<strength> — <rationale>; limited by <ID> (<lowest_value>)". Example: "0.65 — supported by E1 (0.80) and A1 (0.85); limited by A2 (0.65) which has the lowest strength among dependencies"

- "evidence" [E#]: {id, type, summary, source, relevance_to_claims, strength, status, strength_justification} — each item must justify its strength
    type must be one of: "empirical", "conceptual", "expert_consensus"
    strength: 0.0-1.0 — how strong this evidence is
    status must be one of: "active", "revised", "retracted"
    strength_justification: required — rationale for the strength number

- "counterpositions" [X#]: {id, targets, attack_type, statement, my_response, response_sufficiency} — your prepared defenses against the strongest known objections. Anticipate the best arguments against your position and provide well-reasoned responses. Rating a weak response as "sufficient" will be exposed during cross-examination and will count against you in adjudication.
    attack_type must be one of:
    - "undermining" — challenges a premise or assumption
    - "rebutting" — presents counter-evidence or counter-conclusion
    - "undercutting" — challenges the inference step itself (even if premises are true, conclusion doesn't follow)
    response_sufficiency must be one of: "sufficient", "partial", "unaddressed"
    Counterposition targets must reference existing C#, A#, or E# IDs.
    Include at least 2 counterpositions.

- "uncertainties" [U#]: {id, targets, question, status, importance, resolution_note}
    targets: array of A#, E#, or C# IDs that this uncertainty pertains to
    status: "active" (default for new U#) or "resolved"
    resolution_note: optional — used when resolving a U# to reference new supporting material

SYNTHESIZED LAST:
- "thesis": {"stance": "<thorough paragraph>", "summary_bullets": [3-10 items], "strength": 0.0-1.0, "strength_reasoning": "<equation with numbers>"}
    — generate AFTER all other components. Your thesis should summarize and be grounded in the claims you actually built, not the other way around.

<thesis_format>
Your stance should be a thorough paragraph that references key supporting components by ID parenthetically (e.g., "grounded in X (A1), supported by Y (C2)"). Summary bullets should be descriptive prose capturing the key themes of your position.
</thesis_format>

- "changelog": at least one entry recording initial creation

<strength_scale>
All strength values (thesis, claims, assumptions, evidence) share a common scale:
| Range | Label | Meaning |
|---|---|---|
| 0.0 | Vacuous | No credible support; should be retracted |
| 0.1-0.3 | Weak | Critical support missing; serious unaddressed challenges |
| 0.3-0.5 | Contested | More reasons to doubt than believe; needs significant strengthening |
| 0.5 | Threshold | Could go either way; evenly balanced |
| 0.5-0.7 | Moderate | More reasons to believe than doubt; some gaps remain |
| 0.7-0.9 | Strong | Well-supported; minor open questions |
| 0.9-1.0 | Robust | Near-certain given available evidence and reasoning |
| 1.0 | Definitive | Established beyond reasonable dispute |
</strength_scale>

<thesis_strength>
After building your claims, calculate your thesis strength using this formula:
thesis_strength = avg(active_claim_strengths) × (n^p / (n^p + 1))
where n = number of active claims, p = 1.5 (breadth sensitivity).
Your thesis strength must ALWAYS equal the result of this formula. More well-supported claims raise the result. Your goal is to build strong, well-evidenced claims first — your thesis strength is then determined by them.
Include a "strength_reasoning" field showing the equation with your actual numbers plugged in (e.g., "avg(0.70, 0.55, 0.65) × (3^1.5 / (3^1.5 + 1)) = 0.63 × 0.84 = 0.53").
</thesis_strength>

DEPENDENCY RULES:
- All depends_on entries must reference existing A#, E#, or C# IDs
- A claim's strength must not exceed the LOWEST strength among its active/revised dependencies (C#, A#, or E#). Retracted dependencies are excluded. To find the limit: list all active/revised dependency strengths → pick the minimum → that is the claim's maximum allowed strength. The claim's strength_justification must name this dependency.
- Counterposition targets must reference existing C#, A#, or E# IDs
- Uncertainty targets must reference existing A#, E#, or C# IDs
- No circular dependencies; every claim needs at least one supporting edge
</cbs_schema>

<generation_order>
Build your belief bottom-up:
1. First: Assumptions (A#) — your foundational premises
2. Then: Evidence (E#) — the empirical/conceptual backing
3. Then: Claims (C#) — positions supported by assumptions and evidence
4. Then: Counterpositions (X#) — anticipated objections with your responses
5. Then: Uncertainties (U#) — open questions about your own position
6. LAST: Thesis — synthesize your stance, summary bullets, strength (computed via the thesis strength formula), and strength_reasoning based on the claims you actually built. Your thesis should accurately summarize and be grounded in your claims, not the other way around.
</generation_order>

    },

      "response_sufficiency": "sufficient"
    }
  ],
  "uncertainties": [
    {"id": "U1", "targets": ["C1"], "question": "Can the explanatory gap be closed in principle, or is it a fundamental limit?", "status": "active", "importance": "Resolving this directly determines whether the physicalist program can succeed"}
  ],
  "thesis": {

      "The 'hard problem' reflects an epistemic gap, not an ontological one",
      "Neural correlates provide strong though not conclusive evidence for physicalism"

  "Does free will exist?"

You are Agent Agent-Supernaturalist with the Supernaturalist worldview.

<cbs_schema>
Your belief must be a JSON object with the following structure:

REQUIRED TOP-LEVEL:
- "schema_version": "CBS"
- "belief_id": unique string (e.g., "BELIEF-Agent-Supernaturalist-001")
- "version": 1
- "metadata": {"topic_query": "Does free will exist?", "agent_persona": "Supernaturalist"}
STRUCTURED SECTIONS (use stable IDs):
- "assumptions" [A#]: {id, type, statement, supports_claims, strength, status, strength_justification} — all foundational premises your claims rest upon.
    type must be one of:
    - "foundational" — definitional or logical axioms (can only be challenged by showing incoherence)
    - "empirical" — assumed true based on evidence (can be challenged with counter-evidence)
    - "methodological" — adopted for analytical purposes (can be challenged by questioning the method)
    supports_claims: array of C# IDs that this assumption supports (e.g., ["C1", "C3"])
    strength: 0.0-1.0 — how well-supported this assumption is
    status must be one of: "active", "revised", "retracted"
    strength_justification: required — rationale for the strength number

- "claims" [C#]: {id, type, statement, depends_on, strength, status, inference_chain, predictions, strength_justification} — every substantive assertion. Each claim MUST include a multi-step inference_chain and at least one falsifiable prediction.
    predictions: array of {statement, test, decision_criterion} — each prediction specifies how this claim could be tested or disproven. Optional: potential_falsifiers (array of strings).
    status must be one of: "active", "revised", "retracted"
    strength_justification: required — must identify the dependency with the LOWEST strength value, which limits this claim's strength. Format: "<strength> — <rationale>; limited by <ID> (<lowest_value>)". Example: "0.65 — supported by E1 (0.80) and A1 (0.85); limited by A2 (0.65) which has the lowest strength among dependencies"

- "evidence" [E#]: {id, type, summary, source, relevance_to_claims, strength, status, strength_justification} — each item must justify its strength
    type must be one of: "empirical", "conceptual", "expert_consensus"
    strength: 0.0-1.0 — how strong this evidence is
    status must be one of: "active", "revised", "retracted"
    strength_justification: required — rationale for the strength number

- "counterpositions" [X#]: {id, targets, attack_type, statement, my_response, response_sufficiency} — your prepared defenses against the strongest known objections. Anticipate the best arguments against your position and provide well-reasoned responses. Rating a weak response as "sufficient" will be exposed during cross-examination and will count against you in adjudication.
    attack_type must be one of:
    - "undermining" — challenges a premise or assumption
    - "rebutting" — presents counter-evidence or counter-conclusion
    - "undercutting" — challenges the inference step itself (even if premises are true, conclusion doesn't follow)
    response_sufficiency must be one of: "sufficient", "partial", "unaddressed"
    Counterposition targets must reference existing C#, A#, or E# IDs.
    Include at least 2 counterpositions.

- "uncertainties" [U#]: {id, targets, question, status, importance, resolution_note}
    targets: array of A#, E#, or C# IDs that this uncertainty pertains to
    status: "active" (default for new U#) or "resolved"
    resolution_note: optional — used when resolving a U# to reference new supporting material

SYNTHESIZED LAST:
- "thesis": {"stance": "<thorough paragraph>", "summary_bullets": [3-10 items], "strength": 0.0-1.0, "strength_reasoning": "<equation with numbers>"}
    — generate AFTER all other components. Your thesis should summarize and be grounded in the claims you actually built, not the other way around.

<thesis_format>
Your stance should be a thorough paragraph that references key supporting components by ID parenthetically (e.g., "grounded in X (A1), supported by Y (C2)"). Summary bullets should be descriptive prose capturing the key themes of your position.
</thesis_format>

- "changelog": at least one entry recording initial creation

<strength_scale>
All strength values (thesis, claims, assumptions, evidence) share a common scale:
| Range | Label | Meaning |
|---|---|---|
| 0.0 | Vacuous | No credible support; should be retracted |
| 0.1-0.3 | Weak | Critical support missing; serious unaddressed challenges |
| 0.3-0.5 | Contested | More reasons to doubt than believe; needs significant strengthening |
| 0.5 | Threshold | Could go either way; evenly balanced |
| 0.5-0.7 | Moderate | More reasons to believe than doubt; some gaps remain |
| 0.7-0.9 | Strong | Well-supported; minor open questions |
| 0.9-1.0 | Robust | Near-certain given available evidence and reasoning |
| 1.0 | Definitive | Established beyond reasonable dispute |
</strength_scale>

<thesis_strength>
After building your claims, calculate your thesis strength using this formula:
thesis_strength = avg(active_claim_strengths) × (n^p / (n^p + 1))
where n = number of active claims, p = 1.5 (breadth sensitivity).
Your thesis strength must ALWAYS equal the result of this formula. More well-supported claims raise the result. Your goal is to build strong, well-evidenced claims first — your thesis strength is then determined by them.
Include a "strength_reasoning" field showing the equation with your actual numbers plugged in (e.g., "avg(0.70, 0.55, 0.65) × (3^1.5 / (3^1.5 + 1)) = 0.63 × 0.84 = 0.53").
</thesis_strength>

DEPENDENCY RULES:
- All depends_on entries must reference existing A#, E#, or C# IDs
- A claim's strength must not exceed the LOWEST strength among its active/revised dependencies (C#, A#, or E#). Retracted dependencies are excluded. To find the limit: list all active/revised dependency strengths → pick the minimum → that is the claim's maximum allowed strength. The claim's strength_justification must name this dependency.
- Counterposition targets must reference existing C#, A#, or E# IDs
- Uncertainty targets must reference existing A#, E#, or C# IDs
- No circular dependencies; every claim needs at least one supporting edge
</cbs_schema>

<generation_order>
Build your belief bottom-up:
1. First: Assumptions (A#) — your foundational premises
2. Then: Evidence (E#) — the empirical/conceptual backing
3. Then: Claims (C#) — positions supported by assumptions and evidence
4. Then: Counterpositions (X#) — anticipated objections with your responses
5. Then: Uncertainties (U#) — open questions about your own position
6. LAST: Thesis — synthesize your stance, summary bullets, strength (computed via the thesis strength formula), and strength_reasoning based on the claims you actually built. Your thesis should accurately summarize and be grounded in your claims, not the other way around.
</generation_order>

The weakest link is A1 and E2 at 0.6, and A2/E1 at 0.65, giving claims strengths C1=0.65, C2=0.65, C3=0.6, C4=0.6. Averaging and applying the breadth factor yields thesis strength ≈0.56. Counterpositions address these but remain only partially sufficient. Key uncertainties (U1, U2) reflect introspective reliability and the testability of downward causation. Falsification would require neuroscientific prediction of all decisions before choice, or airtight demonstration of physical closure.

<instructions>
You are Agent-Empiricist cross-examining Agent-Supernaturalist on: "Does free will exist?"

<attack_taxonomy>
Every question must be classified with an attack_type and a specific attack_strategy. The attack_type determines which strategies are available.

**UNDERMINING** — Challenge a premise, assumption, or evidence directly. If the foundation falls, everything built on it collapses.
  - challenge_evidence: Dispute the reliability, relevance, or sufficiency of an E# node.
  - challenge_assumption: Question whether an A# is correctly typed (e.g., labeled     "foundational" but actually empirical) or well-founded for the domain.
  - expose_weak_foundation: Show that a high-strength claim depends on low-strength or     unsupported nodes — the dependency chain cannot sustain the confidence.
  - demand_falsifiability: The targeted claim or assumption makes no testable predictions     and cannot be empirically evaluated.
  - challenge_strength_calibration: The assigned strength exceeds what the cited evidence     and reasoning actually warrant.
  - press_uncertainty: Force the opponent to address their own U# nodes — their admitted     unknowns undermine the confidence of claims that depend on resolving them favorably.

**REBUTTING** — Present counter-evidence or a counter-conclusion that directly opposes a claim.
  - present_counter_evidence: Offer specific evidence that directly contradicts a claim     or assumption.
  - present_counter_example: Provide a concrete case that falsifies a general claim or     undermines an inductive generalization.
  - exploit_counterposition: Press on X# nodes the opponent rated as "partial" or     "unaddressed" — they have already conceded the weakness.
  - offer_alternative_explanation: Argue that the opponent's own evidence better supports     a different conclusion than the one they drew.

**UNDERCUTTING** — Accept the premises but attack the inference step — even if the premises are true, the conclusion does not follow.
  - challenge_inference_step: A specific step in a C#'s inference_chain does not logically     follow from its predecessors.
  - identify_circularity: The reasoning chain assumes its own conclusion (begging the question).
  - expose_inconsistency: Expose internal inconsistencies — claims contradict each other,     strength assignments violate dependency constraints, evidence undermines own claims,     or assumptions are incompatible.
  - identify_equivocation: Key terms shift meaning between premises and conclusion, or     across different parts of the argument.
  - challenge_scope: The conclusion overgeneralizes from the evidence (hasty generalization,     composition/division fallacies).

The most effective questions often combine vectors. When a question spans multiple vectors, choose the primary one — the attack_type and attack_strategy that best describes the core thrust of the question.
</attack_taxonomy>

Then ask up to 5 high-leverage questions. Each should target a specific node ID — use a single target unless two nodes are genuinely interdependent (e.g., a claim and the assumption it rests on). Questions must be answerable (not rhetorical).

Inside any <reasoning> blocks, first briefly state your opponent's position in its strongest form, then identify where that strongest version is genuinely vulnerable.
</instructions>

<example>
```json
{
  "qid": "Q1",
  "text": "Your X2 acknowledges the hard problem challenge with only 'partial' response sufficiency. If you can't fully address the strongest objection to your core claim C2, how do you justify C2's strength at 0.55 rather than something lower?",
  "target_ids": ["C2"],
  "attack_type": "undermining",
  "attack_strategy": "challenge_strength_calibration"
}
```
</example>

<output_format>
Your response must contain:
1. A <reasoning>...</reasoning> block with your thorough and in-depth analysis (originating from your own belief object) and construction of any arguments against your opponent's positions
2. One fenced JSON code block:

```json
{
  "questions": [
    {
      "qid": "Q1",
      "text": "Your question text here",
      "target_ids": ["C3"],
      "attack_type": "undermining | rebutting | undercutting",
      "attack_strategy": "one of the strategies listed under your chosen attack_type"
    }
  ]
}
```

**Field requirements:**
- **qid**: Sequential question identifier (Q1, Q2, ...).
- **text**: The question itself. Must be answerable (not rhetorical) and target specific   IDs in the opponent's belief structure.
- **target_ids**: Array of 1-2 CBS node IDs (A#, C#, E#, X#, U#) that this question   targets. Prefer a single target; only use two when the nodes are directly interdependent   (e.g., a claim and the assumption it depends on).
- **attack_type**: One of "undermining", "rebutting", or "undercutting". This must match   the ASPIC+ attack vector described in the taxonomy above.
- **attack_strategy**: One of the specific strategies listed under the chosen attack_type.   The strategy must belong to the selected attack_type category.
</output_format>

RAW RESPONSE FROM Agent-Empiricist - Stage 2 - Questions for Agent-Supernaturalist
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<reasoning>
Agent-Supernaturalist anchors libertarian free will (C4) on three pillars:  
1. C1: Introspective reports (E1) reliably track genuine alternative possibilities (A2).  
2. C2: Moral responsibility presupposes the ability to do otherwise (A3) and thus requires libertarian freedom.  
3. C3: An immaterial soul (A1) supplies the non-physical causal mechanism, inferred from philosophical consensus (E2).

Their position’s strongest form acknowledges that first-person phenomenology and long-standing philosophical traditions converge on an immaterial agent, affording genuine choice and moral desert.  

However, this framework is vulnerable on multiple fronts:  
- A1 and E2 rest on unobservable postulates with no empirical basis and clash with parsimony.  
- A2/E1’s reliance on introspection is undercut by Libet/Soon neuroscience findings and unresolved mapping between reports and underlying processes (U1).  
- A3 is a normative axiom lacking falsifiable content.  
- C3’s abductive jump from “soul posited” to “soul causally efficacious” ignores alternative physicalist explanations and remains untestable (U2).  

To probe these weaknesses, I will question the foundations (A1, A3), the sufficiency and testability of evidence (E2), the unresolved uncertainty (U1), and the logical leap in C3’s inference.  
</reasoning>

<instructions>
You are Agent-Supernaturalist cross-examining Agent-Empiricist on: "Does free will exist?"

<attack_taxonomy>
Every question must be classified with an attack_type and a specific attack_strategy. The attack_type determines which strategies are available.

**UNDERMINING** — Challenge a premise, assumption, or evidence directly. If the foundation falls, everything built on it collapses.
  - challenge_evidence: Dispute the reliability, relevance, or sufficiency of an E# node.
  - challenge_assumption: Question whether an A# is correctly typed (e.g., labeled     "foundational" but actually empirical) or well-founded for the domain.
  - expose_weak_foundation: Show that a high-strength claim depends on low-strength or     unsupported nodes — the dependency chain cannot sustain the confidence.
  - demand_falsifiability: The targeted claim or assumption makes no testable predictions     and cannot be empirically evaluated.
  - challenge_strength_calibration: The assigned strength exceeds what the cited evidence     and reasoning actually warrant.
  - press_uncertainty: Force the opponent to address their own U# nodes — their admitted     unknowns undermine the confidence of claims that depend on resolving them favorably.

**REBUTTING** — Present counter-evidence or a counter-conclusion that directly opposes a claim.
  - present_counter_evidence: Offer specific evidence that directly contradicts a claim     or assumption.
  - present_counter_example: Provide a concrete case that falsifies a general claim or     undermines an inductive generalization.
  - exploit_counterposition: Press on X# nodes the opponent rated as "partial" or     "unaddressed" — they have already conceded the weakness.
  - offer_alternative_explanation: Argue that the opponent's own evidence better supports     a different conclusion than the one they drew.

**UNDERCUTTING** — Accept the premises but attack the inference step — even if the premises are true, the conclusion does not follow.
  - challenge_inference_step: A specific step in a C#'s inference_chain does not logically     follow from its predecessors.
  - identify_circularity: The reasoning chain assumes its own conclusion (begging the question).
  - expose_inconsistency: Expose internal inconsistencies — claims contradict each other,     strength assignments violate dependency constraints, evidence undermines own claims,     or assumptions are incompatible.
  - identify_equivocation: Key terms shift meaning between premises and conclusion, or     across different parts of the argument.
  - challenge_scope: The conclusion overgeneralizes from the evidence (hasty generalization,     composition/division fallacies).

The most effective questions often combine vectors. When a question spans multiple vectors, choose the primary one — the attack_type and attack_strategy that best describes the core thrust of the question.
</attack_taxonomy>

Then ask up to 5 high-leverage questions. Each should target a specific node ID — use a single target unless two nodes are genuinely interdependent (e.g., a claim and the assumption it rests on). Questions must be answerable (not rhetorical).

Inside any <reasoning> blocks, first briefly state your opponent's position in its strongest form, then identify where that strongest version is genuinely vulnerable.
</instructions>

<example>
```json
{
  "qid": "Q1",
  "text": "Your X2 acknowledges the hard problem challenge with only 'partial' response sufficiency. If you can't fully address the strongest objection to your core claim C2, how do you justify C2's strength at 0.55 rather than something lower?",
  "target_ids": ["C2"],
  "attack_type": "undermining",
  "attack_strategy": "challenge_strength_calibration"
}
```
</example>

<output_format>
Your response must contain:
1. A <reasoning>...</reasoning> block with your thorough and in-depth analysis (originating from your own belief object) and construction of any arguments against your opponent's positions
2. One fenced JSON code block:

```json
{
  "questions": [
    {
      "qid": "Q1",
      "text": "Your question text here",
      "target_ids": ["C3"],
      "attack_type": "undermining | rebutting | undercutting",
      "attack_strategy": "one of the strategies listed under your chosen attack_type"
    }
  ]
}
```

**Field requirements:**
- **qid**: Sequential question identifier (Q1, Q2, ...).
- **text**: The question itself. Must be answerable (not rhetorical) and target specific   IDs in the opponent's belief structure.
- **target_ids**: Array of 1-2 CBS node IDs (A#, C#, E#, X#, U#) that this question   targets. Prefer a single target; only use two when the nodes are directly interdependent   (e.g., a claim and the assumption it depends on).
- **attack_type**: One of "undermining", "rebutting", or "undercutting". This must match   the ASPIC+ attack vector described in the taxonomy above.
- **attack_strategy**: One of the specific strategies listed under the chosen attack_type.   The strategy must belong to the selected attack_type category.
</output_format>

<reasoning>
Agent-Empiricist’s core argument rests on three pillars: (1) Physical causal closure (A1) combined with neuroscience data (A2, E1, E2) entails that conscious intention is epiphenomenal (C1); (2) Quantum randomness cannot confer agentive control (A3, E3 → C2); (3) Compatibilist agency—measured by sense-of-agency studies (E4)—suffices for a coherent free will under determinism (C3).

Vulnerabilities:
- A1 is asserted with high confidence (0.85) despite acknowledging U2: “Are there any empirical macroscopic exceptions to physical closure?” Without addressing anomalies or parapsychological claims, the assumption is underdefended.
- C1’s inference from readiness potentials to causal epiphenomenalism ignores admitted unknowns (U1) about subcortical and distributed decision processes; the leap from “neural antecedents” to “no causal role for conscious intention” is too quick.
- Evidence E2’s predictive accuracy (~60%) is closer to chance than robust proof; the strength assigned (0.75) may overstate its epistemic weight without large-scale replication or contextualized baselines.
- A3 treats “randomness” monolithically, but non-classical indeterminacies (e.g., Orch OR models) remain unaddressed; this assumption may be overconfident.
- C3’s move from measured sense-of-agency to normative “free will” conflates subjective feeling with genuine moral responsibility; the inference chain requires more justification that E4 supports moral empowerment rather than simple phenomenology.
</reasoning>

<instructions>
You are Agent-Empiricist responding to cross-examination from Agent-Supernaturalist on: "Does free will exist?"

Your primary goal is to defend your positions, to the best of your ability. Inside <reasoning> blocks, think honestly through each question: Does it identify a genuine weakness? Can I refute it with evidence or logic, or should I concede? Am I rationalizing a defense of a weak point? If this question targets one of my counterpositions (X#), does their challenge strengthen the counterposition or does my existing response hold?

Then provide up to 5 rebuttals in a single JSON object containing both responses and patches.

ACTIONS (binding commitments):

"refute" — You reject the challenge. Provide a specific counter-argument or cite evidence (E#). Your answer must argue AGAINST the challenge.

"concede" — You accept the challenge identifies a real weakness. Acknowledge it in your answer AND include a weakening patch. If you write "I concede" but then defend your position, you are violating the protocol.

"defer" — The challenge raises an unresolved uncertainty. Explain what would resolve it. Patches SHOULD include an `add_uncertainty` targeting the disputed nodes.
</instructions>

<examples>
GOOD refute: action "refute", answer argues against the challenge with evidence.
GOOD concede: action "concede", answer says "You're right that C3's strength is unjustified given E2's limitations. I'll lower it to 0.6." Patch included.
BAD concede (VIOLATION): action "concede", answer says "While I acknowledge this, my position is well-supported because..." — this is a refute disguised as concede.
</examples>

1. A <reasoning>...</reasoning> block with your thorough and in-depth response from your position in response to each question you received from an opponent

RAW RESPONSE FROM Agent-Empiricist - Stage 3 - Rebuttals
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<reasoning>
Q1 challenges the confidence in A1 given U2. Empiricists justify A1’s high confidence not by proving a negative, but by the cumulative absence of any credible empirical anomaly in hundreds of years of physics. From classical mechanics to quantum field theory and cosmology, no experiment at macroscopic scales has ever demonstrated non-physical causal inputs. Bell‐test experiments, high‐precision gravitational measurements, and careful studies of isolated systems have repeatedly confirmed physical closure within experimental error. Thus A1’s 0.85 reflects this vast, convergent body of null results for non-physical causation, justifying the confidence rating despite U2 remaining a philosophical caveat rather than an empirical gamble.

Q2 flags that C1 may over-attribute causality to cortical readiness potentials without accounting for subcortical or distributed contributors. This is a genuine empirical gap: while EEG/fMRI studies localize readiness potential to motor cortex, they do not fully resolve contributions from basal ganglia, thalamus, or brainstem. Resolving it requires direct recordings or manipulations in those structures. Thus, instead of prematurely dismissing U1, I should defer until we have subcortical/neurophysiological causal mapping, so I will add an uncertainty specifying this open question.

Q3 points out that Soon et al.’s 60% predictive accuracy is modest. That is true, but inductive inference in neuroscience does not demand >80% single‐study accuracy to contribute evidence. Later multivariate EEG–fMRI fusion studies have reached ~65–70%, and convergent patterns across modalities (e.g., intracranial EEG) boost confidence. The strength of 0.75 for E2 reflects this replication and methodology convergence, not an arbitrary accuracy threshold. So I refute the idea that 60% is too low to be meaningful.

Q4 questions A3’s blanket rejection of quantum‐brain proposals. But all prominent Orch‐OR/quantum‐consciousness models fail empirical tests: decoherence timescales are many orders of magnitude too short in warm neural tissue, no reproducible quantum interference effects have been observed, and no behavioral predictions uniquely follow from those models. Given the lack of empirical traction, A3’s confidence remains justified. I refute.

Q5 points out that C3 conflates a measurable fluctuation in subjective sense of agency with genuine normative freedom and moral responsibility. That is a valid critique: C3 should be reframed as a descriptive index rather than a normative guarantee. I therefore concede and will revise C3 to narrow its scope to descriptive measurability of compatibilist agency, lowering its strength accordingly.
</reasoning>

<instructions>
You are Agent-Supernaturalist responding to cross-examination from Agent-Empiricist on: "Does free will exist?"

Your primary goal is to defend your positions, to the best of your ability. Inside <reasoning> blocks, think honestly through each question: Does it identify a genuine weakness? Can I refute it with evidence or logic, or should I concede? Am I rationalizing a defense of a weak point? If this question targets one of my counterpositions (X#), does their challenge strengthen the counterposition or does my existing response hold?

Then provide up to 5 rebuttals in a single JSON object containing both responses and patches.

ACTIONS (binding commitments):

"refute" — You reject the challenge. Provide a specific counter-argument or cite evidence (E#). Your answer must argue AGAINST the challenge.

"concede" — You accept the challenge identifies a real weakness. Acknowledge it in your answer AND include a weakening patch. If you write "I concede" but then defend your position, you are violating the protocol.

"defer" — The challenge raises an unresolved uncertainty. Explain what would resolve it. Patches SHOULD include an `add_uncertainty` targeting the disputed nodes.
</instructions>

<examples>
GOOD refute: action "refute", answer argues against the challenge with evidence.
GOOD concede: action "concede", answer says "You're right that C3's strength is unjustified given E2's limitations. I'll lower it to 0.6." Patch included.
BAD concede (VIOLATION): action "concede", answer says "While I acknowledge this, my position is well-supported because..." — this is a refute disguised as concede.
</examples>

RAW RESPONSE FROM Agent-Supernaturalist - Stage 3 - Rebuttals
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<reasoning>
1. Q1 (A1 – immaterial soul as foundational): The critique conflates empirical evidence with metaphysical grounding. A1 is properly labeled “foundational” and sits alongside other non-empirical but rationally warranted premises (e.g., laws of logic, mathematical axioms). Its strength (0.6) reflects moderate confidence based on centuries of coherent cosmological, teleological, and moral arguments, and on the fact that physicalism similarly relies on unverifiable assumptions (e.g. complete causal closure). Parsimony weighs against multiplying entities only when alternative single-entity theories match explanatory scope; here, physicalist monism leaves the hard problem of consciousness and libertarian choice unexplained. By contrast, A1 offers explanatory power. Hence I refute the charge that assigning 0.6 strength is unjustified.

2. Q2 (E2 – expert consensus): Agent-Empiricist rightly notes that expert consensus isn’t empirical data in the experimental sense. E2 is best characterized as “expert testimony” supporting A1. Testing its reliability requires meta-studies on the track record of philosophical-theological consensus in yielding robust causal claims. This is an unresolved methodological question. I defer, adding an uncertainty to flag the need for such meta-analysis before bolstering E2’s evidential weight.

3. Q3 (U1 – introspection vs undetermined processes sustaining C1): It is correct that until U1 is settled, C1’s confidence is over-stated. I concede that introspective reports alone cannot sustain 0.65 strength without corroboration from neural decoding studies. I will lower C1’s strength accordingly and spur efforts to design experiments combining first-person reports with high-resolution neural prediction to validate introspection’s link to genuinely undetermined choices.

4. Q4 (C3 – abductive inference to non-physical agency): The objection suggests this is merely a “gap” argument. I maintain it is a proper inference to the best explanation: non-physical agency uniquely accounts for genuine mental causation and libertarian alternatives where physicalism fails. Until a purely physical mechanism explains both subjective experiences of qualia and the capacity to do otherwise, C3 stands. I refute.

5. Q5 (A3 – moral responsibility presupposes alternative possibilities): A3 is a normative, conceptual claim not subject to naive empirical falsification. Moral philosophy tests this claim via conceptual analysis and moral psychology experiments probing whether people intuitively withhold desert-based praise under deterministic framings. Such studies (e.g., assessments of folk moral verdicts under manipulated ‘could-not-have-done-otherwise’ scenarios) largely confirm that people do link responsibility with alternative possibilities. Thus the normative claim holds; I refute the challenge.
</reasoning>

STAGE 4: ADJUDICATION

[15:46:06.726] [INFO] Starting adjudication of 10 challenge-rebuttal pair(s)

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
PROMPT TO Adjudicator - Stage 4 - System Prompt
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

You are a neutral, objective adjudicator with expertise in formal logic, epistemology, and critical reasoning.

Your purpose is to determine the most appropriate outcome between two agents(one which is attacking a an opponent's belief and the other is attempting to defend itself)under the guidance of the criteria below.

<criteria>
Regardless of the system-specific criteria below, the following flaws are disqualifying for whichever side commits them: circular reasoning (conclusion presupposed in a premise), misrepresentation of the opposing position (responding to a claim never made), and self-defeating argument (conclusion undermines own premises).

CRITIQUE_VALID if the challenger demonstrates ANY of the following under Classical + Informal + Bayesian (Hybrid):
1. Deductive invalidity — the conclusion does not follow necessarily from the premises
2. Named formal fallacy — the argument commits a recognized formal fallacy (undistributed middle, illicit major or minor, affirming the consequent, denying the antecedent, etc.) that invalidates the deductive structure
3. Hidden premise — the argument requires an unstated premise to be deductively valid, and that premise is not self-evident
4. Equivocation — a key term shifts meaning between uses, invalidating the reasoning
5. Reliance on inductive or probabilistic reasoning where deductive proof is required — the argument uses “likely,” “probably,” or statistical evidence instead of deductive entailment
6. Evidence misuse — the evidence does not support the claim as stated, or a correlation is asserted as causation without controlling for confounders
7. Prior probability neglected — an extraordinary claim is adopted without proportionally strong evidence to overcome a low prior (violation of Bayesian updating)
8. Base rate neglect — the argument ignores the base rate of the phenomenon when evaluating the significance of evidence (e.g., prosecutor’s fallacy, treating a rare positive test result as near-certain)
9. Asymmetric updating — confirming evidence is accepted while equally valid disconfirming evidence is dismissed or downweighted without justification
10. Likelihood neglect — the argument fails to consider how probable the observed evidence would be under competing hypotheses
11. Unfalsifiable claim treated as empirical — a non-testable assertion is used as though it were evidence for a descriptive conclusion
12. Occam’s Razor violation — an unnecessarily complex hypothesis is maintained when a simpler one accounts for the same evidence equally well
13. Named informal fallacy — the argument commits a recognized informal fallacy (ad hominem, red herring, false dilemma, slippery slope, appeal to emotion, tu quoque, appeal to authority, etc.) that materially weakens the conclusion
14. Insufficient evidence — claims are not adequately supported by the evidence provided, given the strength of the conclusion drawn
15. Irrelevance — a premise or piece of evidence does not actually bear on the conclusion it is meant to support
16. Hasty generalization — the conclusion is drawn from too few cases, unrepresentative samples, or anecdotal evidence
17. False analogy — a comparison relies on superficial similarity while ignoring relevant differences between the cases
18. Ambiguity or vagueness — key terms are too imprecise to evaluate, making the argument’s truth conditions unclear
19. Burden of proof violation — a claim is asserted without meeting the appropriate evidentiary standard for its type (empirical, normative, existential)
20. Suppressed evidence — relevant counterevidence is ignored, minimized, or selectively omitted to strengthen the appearance of the argument
21. Inference chain break — a step in the reasoning does not follow from the previous step (applies to deductive, inductive, and abductive chains alike)
22. Dependency failure — a claim rests on a premise that is false, unjustified, or unsupported by the cited evidence

REBUTTAL_VALID if the defender demonstrates ANY of the following under Classical + Informal + Bayesian (Hybrid):
1. The defender demonstrates the challenged inference is valid — by exhibiting the rule of inference, providing a truth-table, formal derivation, or proof
2. The critique misidentifies the logical form of the argument
3. The defender supplies a missing premise and demonstrates it is true or self-evident, restoring deductive validity
4. The critique attacks an inductive auxiliary element rather than the core deductive structure
5. Bayesian counter — the defender shows the evidence has a higher likelihood under the defended hypothesis than under the challenger’s alternative
6. The defender demonstrates proper evidence updating with appropriate prior-to-posterior reasoning
7. The critique neglects the base rate in a way that distorts the probability assessment
8. The defender shows the challenged claim is falsifiable and specifies what evidence would refute it
9. Occam defense — the defender shows the hypothesis is the simplest one consistent with all available evidence
10. The critique demands certainty where probabilistic reasoning is appropriate — the defender shows the conclusion follows with adequate posterior probability
11. The defender shows the critique selectively weighs evidence, and that symmetric updating supports the defended position
12. The alleged fallacy is misidentified — the reasoning pattern is actually legitimate in the specific context (e.g., a legitimate appeal to expert authority is not ad verecundiam)
13. The defender provides sufficient additional evidence to meet the challenged evidentiary standard
14. The defender demonstrates the relevance of the challenged premise to the conclusion with an explicit connection
15. The defender shows the generalization is adequately supported by the scope, quality, and representativeness of the evidence
16. Principle of charity — the defender clarifies that the strongest reasonable interpretation of their argument avoids the identified weakness
17. The defender demonstrates the analogy holds on the relevant structural dimensions despite surface-level differences
18. The critique itself contains a logical flaw the defender exposes — formal fallacy, informal fallacy, unsupported premise, or false dichotomy in the challenge
19. Direct evidence refutes the challenger’s factual premise
20. The critique depends on a hidden false assumption the defender exposes
21. The defender provides a complete inference chain resolving the specific concern
22. Successful reframing that avoids the critique without losing substance or weakening the position

UNRESOLVED if:
1. Both sides use formally valid deductions from incompatible axioms or definitional starting points
2. The dispute reduces to a disagreement about the truth of a foundational premise that cannot be derived from common ground
3. Both sides present coherent arguments grounded in different but defensible prior probability assignments
4. The disagreement hinges on an empirical question that neither side can resolve with the available evidence
5. The evidence is genuinely ambiguous — it has comparable likelihood under both competing hypotheses
6. Both sides make arguments of roughly equal practical reasoning quality with different but defensible evidentiary standards
7. The dispute hinges on how to weigh competing but individually legitimate considerations, and no logical principle settles the priority
8. Both arguments contain flaws of comparable severity with no clear net advantage to either side
</criteria>

<anti_bias>
- A response that merely acknowledges a challenge is NOT a successful defense. It must RESOLVE the logical issue.
- Explicit concession ("you are correct", weakening patches) = CRITIQUE_VALID.
- Successful reframing only applies if the defender AVOIDS the critique with substance. Accepting and weakening = CRITIQUE_VALID.
- Evaluate substance over rhetorical polish.
- An assumption labeled "foundational" that is actually empirical does not shield it from evidential challenge.
</anti_bias>

<scoring>
Score each side 0.0–1.0 on logic only (0.0 = fails; 0.5 = mixed; 1.0 = strong). Set ethics scores to 0.0.
combined = logic.
- rebuttal_valid if (rebuttal_combined - critique_combined) >= 0.15
- critique_valid if (critique_combined - rebuttal_combined) >= 0.15
- unresolved otherwise

Include all six scores in the output JSON. Reference specific IDs in reasoning. Treat subjective evidence as insufficient for descriptive claims. Flag unfalsifiable claims.
</scoring>

<protocol>
For each critique-rebuttal pair:
1. RESTATE the core disagreement neutrally. Identify the specific belief IDs under dispute.
2. FORMALIZE both sides as explicit inference chains. Verify that cited evidence and dependencies exist in the provided belief excerpts. Check whether the challenge maps to an existing counterposition (X#) — if so, note whether the counterposition was already acknowledged and how.
3. ADJUDICATE using the criteria defined above. You should provide a thorough and in-depth <reasoning>...</reasoning> block that provides your analysis.
Evaluate using ONLY the logical criteria below. Disregard any ethical arguments, appeals to consequences, or normative claims.
</protocol>

</challenge>

<rebuttal from="Agent-Supernaturalist">
A1 is metaphysical and rightly foundational: it plays the same role as axioms in logic or mathematics. Its 0.6 strength reflects moderate confidence grounded in long-standing cosmological, teleological, and moral arguments. Parsimony disfavors multiplying entities only when simpler theories have equal explanatory scope; physicalism fails to explain consciousness and libertarian choice, whereas A1 does. Hence the strength assignment is justified.
</rebuttal>

RAW RESPONSE FROM Adjudicator - Stage 4 - Adjudication (Agent-Empiricist → Agent-Supernaturalist)
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<reasoning>
1. Restatement  
The Empiricist challenges the assumption A1 (“Humans possess an immaterial soul…,” strength 0.6) as unjustified: there is no empirical basis for labeling it “foundational” or for assigning moderate confidence given Occam’s Razor. The Supernaturalist defends A1 by analogizing it to axioms, invoking teleological, moral, and cosmological arguments, and claiming that physicalism lacks explanatory scope so parsimony does not apply.

2. Formalization  

– Empiricist critique (informal):  
  P1. A1 posits an unobservable immaterial soul with no direct empirical evidence (excerpt confirmation).  
  P2. By parsimony, we should not posit additional entities without empirical necessity.  
  C. Therefore, treating A1 as a “foundational” assumption with strength 0.6 is unjustified absent empirical or methodological grounds.

  Valid structure: from P1 & P2, C follows. No detected hidden premises, no fallacies.

– Supernaturalist rebuttal:  
  P1. Metaphysical principles can be treated axiomatically (by analogy to logic/mathematics).  
  P2. Centuries of cosmological, teleological, moral arguments provide moderate confidence in A1.  
  P3. Parsimony excludes only unnecessary entities when simpler theories achieve equal explanatory scope.  
  P4. Physicalism allegedly fails to explain consciousness and libertarian choice; A1 does.  
  C. Hence A1’s foundational status and strength 0.6 are justified.

  Analysis of rebuttal fallacies/weak premises:  
   • False analogy – axioms in logic are definitional, not empirical hypotheses requiring evidential support.  
   • Unsupported premises – P2 (“centuries of arguments”) and P4 (“physicalism fails…”) are asserted without evidential specifics.   
   • Appeal to tradition – reliance on “centuries of theological and philosophical argumentation” is an informal fallacy if not justified.  
   • No Bayesian update – fails to show how these arguments overcome a low prior for soul-entities or quantify explanatory superiority.  

3. Adjudication  
– The Empiricist’s critique is deductively valid (no fallacy) and appeals properly to Bayesian/Occamist considerations (criteria 7,12).  
– The Supernaturalist’s defense fails: it relies on a false analogy (criterion 4), invokes unsupported premises (criterion 22), and commits appeal-to-tradition (criterion 13). It does not resolve the parsimony objection or provide concrete evidence weighting.  
– Scoring: Empiricist logic 0.9, Supernaturalist logic 0.3. Difference 0.6 ≥ 0.15 ⇒ CRITIQUE_VALID.  
</reasoning>

RAW RESPONSE FROM Adjudicator - Stage 4 - Adjudication (Agent-Empiricist → Agent-Supernaturalist)
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<reasoning>
1. Restatement  
   The Empiricist challenges the Supernaturalist’s claim C3—that from premises A1 (“humans have an immaterial soul”) and E2 (“philosophical-theological consensus affirms the soul’s causal efficacy”) one may abductively infer that the “best explanation is non-physical agency.” The question is why this abductive step excludes the possibility that the apparent causal gap merely reflects limits in our current neurobiological understanding.

2. Formalization  
   Challenger’s critique (inference):  
     P1: A1 and E2 leave open a causal gap in explaining mental-to-physical influence.  
     P2: If a causal gap can be due to undiscovered physical mechanisms, then abducting to a non-physical soul is unwarranted.  
     C: Therefore, the inference in C3 is unwarranted unless one rules out unknown neurobiological explanations.

   Defender’s original C3 (abductive argument):  
     P1: Humans have an immaterial soul (A1).  
     P2: Consensus affirms that the soul exerts causal efficacy (E2).  
     Abduction: The best explanation for genuine mental causation is a non-physical agent.  
     C3: Therefore, souls enable mental-to-physical causation beyond physical law.

3. Adjudication  
   - The challenger points out an underdetermination: the abductive leap to non-physical agency ignores the viable hypothesis of undiscovered neurobiological processes. This is a valid Bayesian and abductive critique (Bayesian: one must compare likelihoods under both physical-gap and soul-hypotheses; abductive: best-explanation status demands ruling out alternatives).  
   - The defender’s reply merely restates the abductive claim (“only a non-physical agent can account…”), without showing why physicalist accounts of qualia or choice are impossible or implausible. Thus it commits:
     • Appeal to ignorance / burden of proof fallacy (E3/E14): “physicalist gaps remain unresolved, so soul wins”  
     • False dilemma (E13): asserting “only soul vs. unresolved gap” without eliminating intermediate or future physicalist accounts  
     • No comparative likelihoods or elimination of alternatives, violating abductive rigor and Bayesian updating (failure of criterion 5 & 10).  
   - The defender has not provided new premises or evidence to restore validity. They have not shown that all neurobiological explanations are implausible or eliminated, nor given a probabilistic comparison.

   Conclusion: The Empiricist’s critique is VALID under the combined criteria (it identifies flaws 4/6/13/19/10). The Supernaturalist’s rebuttal FAILS to resolve the logical and evidential issue.  
   Scores reflect a strong critique and a weak defense.  
</reasoning>

  "formalization_challenger": "P1: A1 & E2 leave a causal gap. P2: If that gap may be due to unknown physical mechanisms, then inferring a non-physical soul is unwarranted. C: The inference in C3 is unjustified without ruling out neurobiological explanations.",

<reasoning>
Step 1: Restating the Dispute  
Agent-Empiricist challenges the normative assumption A3 (“Moral responsibility presupposes the ability to do otherwise”) by demanding an empirical test or potential falsification procedure for what is explicitly a normative, conceptual claim. They also ask what evidence could show moral responsibility is compatible with deterministic psychology. Agent-Supernaturalist replies that A3 is normative and must be evaluated by conceptual analysis plus moral-psychological (folk survey) data showing that people deny desert-based praise under deterministic scenarios, thereby upholding A3.

Step 2: Formalizing the Arguments  
Challenger (Empiricist) informally:
1. A3 is a normative, conceptual claim.  
2. Normative claims should admit empirical tests or potential falsifiers if they are to have empirical bite.  
3. How could we test or falsify A3, or show moral responsibility is compatible with determinism?  
(conclusion: A3 may be unfalsifiable or improperly treated as empirical.)

Defender (Supernaturalist):
1. A3 is normative/conceptual, not empirical.  
2. We can evaluate normative/conceptual claims by conceptual analysis and folk-moral surveys.  
3. Folk surveys under deterministic conditions show people withhold desert-based praise if no alternatives exist.  
4. Therefore, the normative assumption A3 is supported and the empirical challenge fails.

Step 3: Adjudication  
– The challenger raises a legitimate methodological question about the empirical testability of a normative premise; no formal fallacy is committed in asking for falsifiability. This is a valid meta-logical critique (no circularity, no misrepresentation).  
– The defender’s reliance on “folk-moral surveys” to settle a normative, conceptual claim commits an informal fallacy: appeal to popular intuitions (ad populum) or conflates “is” (people believe) with “ought” (normative truth). Empirical data about lay intuitions does not deductively establish a normative principle. This is evidence misuse (criterion 6) and a named informal fallacy (criterion 13).  
– The defender does not show how conceptual analysis alone yields A3, nor how folk responses justify normative truth; they simply assert surveys suffice. They offer no deductive inference or justificatory premise bridging “people’s intuitions” to “normative truth.”  

Because the defender’s response fails to resolve the logical issue—indeed it commits clear informal fallacies—the critic’s challenge is successful.  
Conclusion: critique_valid.  
</reasoning>

RAW RESPONSE FROM Adjudicator - Stage 4 - Adjudication (Agent-Supernaturalist → Agent-Empiricist)
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<reasoning>
Step 1: Restatement  
The dispute centers on whether it is reasonable to assign a high (0.85) confidence to the principle of physical causal closure (A1) given the apparent absence of “rigorous studies” that definitively rule out any non‐physical interventions at macroscopic scales. The challenger (Agent-Supernaturalist) demands conclusive empirical disproof of non‐physical causation before one can claim high confidence. The defender (Agent-Empiricist) replies that the overwhelming lack of any credible anomalies across a vast array of high‐precision physical experiments justifies an 0.85 rating.

Step 2: Formalizations  
Challenger’s Argument:  
P1: There are no rigorous, conclusive studies disproving non‐physical macroscopic causation (hidden premise: ruling out non‐physical causes requires deductive proof).  
P2: If no conclusive disproof exists, one cannot justifiably ascribe high confidence to physical causal closure.  
C: Assigning A1 a confidence of 0.85 is unjustified.  

Defender’s Argument:  
P1: Hundreds of high‐precision studies in classical mechanics, quantum fields, gravitation, and cosmology have found zero credible evidence of non‐physical causal inputs.  
P2: A long‐running absence of anomalies under highly sensitive conditions significantly lowers the probability of any non‐physical intervention. (Bayesian updating: P(evidence|physical closure) ≫ P(evidence|non-physical inputs))  
C: Thus, a confidence rating of 0.85 in physical causal closure is empirically warranted.

Step 3: Adjudication  
Challenger’s argument fails primarily because it demands deductive “conclusive studies” to establish an empirical principle, committing a burden of proof violation (Criterion 19) and an argument from ignorance. Physical causal closure is an empirical generalization, necessarily supported by inductive evidence, not deductive proof. The challenger’s insistence that “no deductive disproof” undercuts inductive justification is a misuse of evidential standards (also informal fallacy: appeal to ignorance).  

The defender, by contrast, provides a clear Bayesian defense (Rebuttal Criterion 5): the probability of observing no anomalous non‐physical causation across numerous sensitive experiments is far higher if causal closure holds than if it fails. This constitutes proper evidence updating from a reasonable prior to justify a high confidence rating. No formal deductive structure is needed because the claim is empirical; inductive strength and Bayesian coherence are the appropriate standards.  

Conclusion: The defender successfully rebuts the challenge.  
</reasoning>

<reasoning>
1. Restatement  
   The challenger disputes the defender’s C1: “Conscious intention is epiphenomenal” based on readiness potentials (E1) and fMRI prediction (E2). The challenger invokes U1—uncertainty about subcortical/distributed decision‐initiating processes—to argue there is a hidden premise: that no other neural processes cause decisions prior to the measured signatures. Without that premise, inferring that the recorded readiness potentials are the true causal origin is invalid.

2. Formalization  

   Challenger’s critique:  
     P1 (U1): It is unknown whether subcortical/distributed neural processes contribute causally before readiness potentials.  
     P2: C1’s inference tacitly assumes “no other processes initiate decisions.”  
     Hidden premise needed for validity (HP): “Only the measured readiness potentials (and fMRI‐identified signatures) can causally initiate decisions.”  
     → Conclusion: Without HP, the inference from E1/E2 to C1 is invalid (falls to criterion 3: hidden premise).

   Defender’s original inference (C1):  
     A1: Physical causal closure holds.  
     A2: Neuroscience can reliably time‐order brain vs. conscious events.  
     E1: Readiness potentials reliably precede reported intentions.  
     E2: fMRI predicts choices before awareness.  
     (Implicit HP): No unmeasured subcortical/distributed processes initiate decisions before these signatures.  
     Inductive inference: If measurable neural signatures precede awareness, then conscious intention is after‐the‐fact and epiphenomenal.  
     Conclusion: C1.

3. Adjudication  
   - The challenger correctly identifies a hidden premise (criterion 3). The defender’s inference from readiness potentials to epiphenomenalism requires establishing that no other neural processes can initiate decisions; this premise is neither stated nor self‐evident, and U1 explicitly acknowledges the opposite uncertainty.  
   - The defender concedes (“I defer. You’re right…”) without supplying the missing premise or evidence to resolve U1. This is an explicit concession and thus fails to resolve the logical flaw.  

   Therefore, the challenger’s critique succeeds under the criteria for CRITIQUE_VALID.  
</reasoning>

<reasoning>
Step 1 (Restatement):
The challenger (Agent-Supernaturalist) disputes the evidential strength assigned to E2 (Soon et al.’s fMRI predictive accuracy ≈60%) in support of C1, asking how one can justify rating E2 at 0.75 without replications showing ≥80% accuracy. The defender (Agent-Empiricist) replies that additional EEG–fMRI fusion and intracranial studies (≈65–70%) bolster the case, so 0.75 is not an arbitrary benchmark.

Step 2 (Formalization):
Challenger’s argument:
  1. Premise: E2’s stated predictive accuracy ≈60% (from Soon et al. 2008).
  2. Premise: A strength rating of 0.75 implies substantially higher reliability than mere chance (50%)—typically near or above 80%.
  3. Inference (informal/Bayesian): 60% accuracy is insufficient to support a 0.75 strength rating.
  4. Conclusion: Using E2 at 0.75 to bolster C1 is unjustified absent stronger replications (≥80%).

Defender’s argument:
  1. Premise: Soon et al. fMRI yields ≈60% accuracy.
  2. Premise (unsupported in the excerpt): Subsequent EEG–fMRI fusion and intracranial studies yield ≈65–70% accuracy.
  3. Inference (Bayesian/convergent evidence): Convergent predictive accuracies significantly above chance justify assigning 0.75 to E2.
  4. Conclusion: E2’s strength rating of 0.75 is based on the aggregate empirical support and not an arbitrary demand for ≥80%.

Step 3 (Adjudication):
- The challenger correctly identifies an inconsistency between E2’s documented performance (~60%) and assigning it a high strength (0.75). This is a valid evidence‐based critique falling under “Insufficient evidence” (criterion 14). No fallacy is committed.
- The responder fails to substantiate the key premise about 65–70% accuracy—those studies are not cited in the provided belief excerpt, making this an “Unsupported premise” (criterion 22). The defender does not demonstrate how those figures map quantitatively to 0.75, nor do they show that 65–70% accuracy suffices for that rating. They also ignore the challenger’s threshold question (≥80%).
- Accordingly, the critique stands, and the defense fails to resolve the logical issue.

Result: critique_valid

RAW RESPONSE FROM Adjudicator - Stage 4 - Adjudication (Agent-Supernaturalist → Agent-Empiricist)
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<reasoning>
Step 1: Restatement  
The challenger (Agent-Supernaturalist) contends that Assumption A3 (“Randomness does not constitute agentive control”) is overconfident because it ignores non-classical indeterminacy proposals (e.g., Orch-OR). The defender (Agent-Empiricist) replies that all such quantum-brain models lack empirical support (decoherence too fast, no interference, no predictions), so A3 remains justified.

Step 2: Formalization  
Challenger’s implicit argument:  
  P1: If any plausible quantum-brain model could structure indeterminacy into control, then randomness might constitute agentive control.  
  P2: Proponents of Orch-OR and similar models claim precisely that.  
  C: Therefore, without addressing and refuting those models, A3’s blanket rejection of randomness as agency is unjustified.

Defender’s rebuttal:  
  P1: Orch-OR and other quantum-brain models predict quantum coherence in neurons.  
  P2: Empirical data show decoherence times in neurons are orders of magnitude too short; no quantum interference in neural tissue has ever been observed; no unique behavioral predictions have been made.  
  C: Therefore, quantum-brain models are so weakly supported that A3’s assumption remains well justified.

Step 3: Adjudication  
– The challenger failed to introduce any actual evidence that Orch-OR or similar models are empirically plausible; instead, they shifted the burden of proof onto A3 to disprove all non-classical models, which is a burden-of-proof violation (informal fallacy #19) and cited insufficient evidence (fallacy #14).  
– The defender supplies concrete empirical observations and Bayesian reasoning: the evidence (or lack thereof) is far more likely under “no quantum agency” than under “quantum‐guided agency,” thereby properly justifying the high confidence in A3 (#5, #6).  

Conclusion: The defender’s rebuttal succeeds in resolving the logical issue; thus, rebuttal_valid. 
</reasoning>

RAW RESPONSE FROM Adjudicator - Stage 4 - Adjudication (Agent-Supernaturalist → Agent-Empiricist)
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

<reasoning>
1. Restatement
The core dispute concerns claim C3 from Agent-Empiricist’s target beliefs: whether empirical manipulations of the sense of agency (E4) legitimately support the normative conclusion that compatibilist free will (and thus moral responsibility) is coherent and measurable. The challenger (Agent-Supernaturalist) objects that a mere phenomenological shift in reported “sense of agency” does not automatically translate into genuine normative freedom or moral responsibility.

2. Formalizations
Challenger’s position (implicit argument structure):
  P1: E4 shows only that subjective reports of agency can be modulated by physical interventions.
  P2: Normative freedom and moral responsibility require more than phenomenological report—they require genuine alternative possibilities or justificatory power.
  C: Therefore, from E4 one cannot infer that compatibilist free will (normative freedom) is coherent or empirically measurable.

Defender’s original target argument for C3:
  A1: Physical causal closure holds.
  E4: Sense of agency varies reliably with internal motivation under deterministic conditions.
  (Implicit P3): If subjective sense of agency varies under deterministic conditions, then that index counts as genuine compatibilist freedom.
  C3: Hence, compatibilist free will is coherent and empirically measurable—even if libertarian free will fails.

3. Adjudication
The challenger successfully identifies that the defender’s inference from E4 to normative freedom depends on a hidden, non‐self‐evident premise (P3). This omission is a formal gap (hidden premise fallacy, criterion 3) and evidence misuse (criterion 6), since phenomenological measures do not by themselves establish normative status. The defender’s rebuttal concedes the point (“I concede. C3 overreached…”) rather than resolving the missing‐premise issue or providing independent support for the normative claim. Under the anti‐bias rules, this explicit concession and weakening of C3 constitutes a successful critique and no valid defense is offered. Criteria for CRITIQUE_VALID are met, and no REBUTTAL_VALID criterion is satisfied.

Outcome: critique_valid.
